Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
12 min
Testimony Based on Possession: Hanafi Ruling and Shafi'i Dispute
Explains Hanafi ruling and response to Shafi'i on possession testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on testimony by possession, explain the condition of certainty added by Imam Abu Yusuf, and evaluate the Shafi'i objection using the parity argument of qiyas khafi.

Core Idea
Mere possession testifies ownership unless context contradicts; witness must have genuine belief.

This passage presents a key debate between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: can a witness testify that someone owns an object simply because they saw it in their possession? The Hanafi school, as recorded in Imam Muhammad's matn, says yes—because possession is the ultimate outward sign of ownership. Imam Abu Yusuf adds a condition of certainty in the witness's heart, citing the hadith 'When you know it like the sun...'. The Shafi'i school objects that possession alone is ambiguous; the possessor might be a trustee. The Hanafi response uses a subtle analogy (qiyas khafi) to uphold the original ruling. In this lesson, we will trace the legal reasoning step by step and see how the Hanafi school defends its position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position from the matn
Identify the ruling as stated by Imam Muhammad: it is permissible to testify that a person is the owner of an item seen in their possession, provided there is no one disputing the possession.
The matn does not explicitly mention certainty; that comes from Imam Abu Yusuf.
2
Identify Imam Abu Yusuf's additional condition
Imam Abu Yusuf requires that the witness have certainty in their heart that the possessor is the owner, based on the hadith 'When you know it like the sun, then testify; otherwise, leave it.' This is reconciled as an interpretation of the matn.
Later Hanafi scholars treat this as the mufta bihi position.
3
Present the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that possession alone is insufficient because possession can be either ownership possession or trust possession (amanah). Only possession coupled with disposal (tasarruf) indicates ownership clearly.
Some Hanafi scholars also held this view initially.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response via qiyas khafi
The author of al-Hidayah responds: if possession has two types (trust/ownership), then disposal also has two types (direct/representative). If the possibility of trust possession invalidates testimony, then the possibility of representative disposal (e.g., agent) should equally invalidate testimony based on disposal. Since the law does not consider the latter, it should not consider the former.
This is a parity argument (qiyas with a hidden co-relation).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, a witness can testify to ownership by mere possession (with certainty) even without seeing disposal. Under Shafi'i law, disposal must also be witnessed. The practical difference arises in cases where a person holds an item but does not use it openly.
Example: a watch worn by a friend – Hanafi accepts, Shafi'i may reject unless the friend is also seen selling or giving it.

ا۔ امانت کا قبضہ
-۲ ملک کا قبضہ
یعنی جس طرح آدمی مالک ہونے کی وجہ سے قبضہ کرتا ہے اسی طرح امین ہونے کی وجہ سے قبضہ کرتا ہے پس نفس قبضہ، قابض کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ہوسکتا ہے کہ اس کا قبضہ مالکانہ نہ ہو بلکہ امانت کے طور پر ہو اور جب نفس قبضہ قابض کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل نہیں ہے تو محض قبضہ دیکھ کر قابض کے مالک ہونے کی گواہی دینا بھی جائز نہ ہوگا ہاں اگر قبضہ کے ساتھ قابض شی، مقبوض میں تصرف بھی کرتا ہے تو یہ قبضہ مع التصرف اس کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل ہوگا کیونکہ تصرف کا مجاز مالک ہوتا ہے نہ کہ امین پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ خالص قبضہ قابض کی ملکیت کی دلیل نہیں ہے بلکہ قبضہ مع التصرف اسکے مالک ہونے کی دلیل ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جواب دیتے ہوئے فرمایا ہے کہ اگر قبضہ کی دو قسمیں ہیں تو تصرف کی بھی دو قسمیں ہیں کیونکہ انسان کبھی تو بذات خود اپنے لئے تصرف کرتا ہے اور کبھی دوسرے کا نائب ہو کر تصرف کرتا ہے جیسے وکیل اور مضارب اپنے لئے تصرف نہیں کرتے بلکہ متوکل اور رب المال کے لئے تصرف کرتے ہیں پس اگر قبضہ بلا تصرف کو ملکیت کی دلیل قرار نہ دینے میں قبضہ امانت کا احتمال معتبر ہے تو ہمارے بیان کردہ احتمال ( نیابت تصرف) کی بنا پر تصرف بھی ملکیت کی دلیل نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر نیابت تصرف کا احتمال معتبر نہیں ہے تو قبضہ امانت کا احتمال بھی معتبر نہ ہوگا اور جب قبضہ امانت کا احتمال معتبر نہیں ہے تو خالی قبضہ ، قابض کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل ہوگا اور محض قبضہ دیکھ کر قابض کی ملکیت کی گواہی دینا جائز ہوگا۔
Chapter of Testimony. Ashraf al-Bidaya Volume Ten: He saw it in the possession of another person. And the first possessor claimed against the second possessor that he is the owner of this thing, then it is permissible for this observer to testify that the claimant is the owner of this thing. Its evidence is that possession is the last level of something from which it can be inferred that the possessor is its owner, meaning for the witness, possession is the apparent proof for recognizing ownership, provided there is no disputant concerning the possession. That is, possession apparently indicates that the possessor is the owner of the possessed thing because purchase, gift, charity, inheritance, etc. — whatever the causes of ownership, ultimately possession indicates ownership. For example, Khalid observed that Hamid bought a pen from Shahid; then Hamid's (buyer's) ownership will be known when the seller's ownership is known. And the seller's ownership will be known from his possession: that is, due to purchase, the buyer can only be the owner if the seller himself is also the owner, and the seller's ownership will apparently be known when the seller is in possession of it. Similarly, gift is undoubtedly a cause of ownership, but the donee's ownership will only be established when the donor himself is the owner of the gifted thing, and the donor's ownership will apparently be known from possession. Similarly, charity is a cause of ownership, but from giving charity, the poor person's ownership will be established when it is known that the charity giver himself is also the owner of that thing, and the charity giver's ownership will apparently be known from possession. Similarly, inheritance is certainly a cause of ownership, but the heir will only become the owner of the inherited property when it is known that the owner of that property was the deceased, and the deceased's ownership will be known when it is known that he was in possession of that property during his life. In any case, since among all causes of ownership, in the final analysis, possession is the apparent proof of ownership, it is permissible to testify merely by seeing possession that the possessor is the owner of the possessed thing. However, at the time of testimony, he should not say, 'I testify that so-and-so is the owner of this thing because I saw this thing in his possession,' i.e., he should not mention the reason and explanation before the judge, because possession is the apparent proof of ownership, and an apparent proof is sufficient for giving testimony but not sufficient for the judge's verdict. It is narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf that in testifying to the possessor's ownership by seeing possession, it is also a condition that the witness becomes certain in his heart that this thing is the possession of this possessor. Its evidence is that giving testimony is permitted only when the witness has full knowledge and certainty of the matter. This is because of the hadith: 'When you know it like the sun, then testify; otherwise, leave it.' But when full knowledge and certainty are not possible, then recourse is taken to the heart's testimony, i.e., at least the heart bears witness that the possessed thing is the possessor's property, because possession indicates the possessor's ownership in that it gives the impression of ownership. But if it does not enter the heart of the witness that the possessor could be its owner, then the witness will not even have the impression of the possessor's ownership. And when the witness does not have the impression of the possessor's ownership, then possession will not indicate the possessor's ownership. So it is established that mere possession is not sufficient for testifying to the possessor's ownership; rather, along with possession, it is necessary for the witness to have the entry of this matter into his heart that the possessor is the owner of the possessed thing. For this reason, if someone sees an expensive pearl in the hand of a street sweeper, or sees a book in the possession of an ignorant person whose ancestors included no scholar or literate person, it is not permissible for that observer to testify that the street sweeper or ignorant person is the owner. From this, it is understood that mere possession is not sufficient for testifying to ownership; rather, it is necessary for the witness to develop the inclination in his heart that the possessor is the owner. The author of al-Hidayah said: The view of the Hanafi scholars is that the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf is an interpretation of Imam Muhammad's absolute narration, i.e., the issue of the text. That is, Imam Muhammad said in the text only that if there is possession, it is permissible to testify to ownership. But it is possible that its meaning is that this thing enters his heart that his possession is as owner, then it is permissible to testify. So if this is the meaning of the text stated by Imam Muhammad, then the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf would be the interpretation of that absolute text, and the condition for the witness to have that certainty would be agreed upon by all. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The proof of ownership is that possession which is accompanied by disposal (tasarruf). That is, if the possessor disposes of the possessed thing, then his possession is proof of his ownership, and it will be permissible for the witness to testify to his ownership based on possession with disposal. But if he does not dispose, then possession without disposal is not proof of ownership, and it will not be permissible for the witness to testify that the possessor is the owner based on that possession. This is also the view of some of our Hanafi scholars. Because possession is of two types: 1. Possession of trust (amanah), 2. Possession of ownership. That is, just as a person takes possession due to being the owner, similarly he takes possession due to being a trustee. Therefore, mere possession is not proof that the possessor is the owner, because it is possible that his possession is not as owner but as trust. And when mere possession is not proof that the possessor is the owner, then it is also not permissible to testify that the possessor is the owner merely by seeing possession. However, if along with possession the possessor also disposes of the possessed thing, then this possession with disposal is proof of his ownership, because the one who disposes is the owner, not the trustee. So it is established that pure possession is not proof of the possessor's ownership; rather, possession with disposal is proof of his ownership. The author of al-Hidayah responded to Imam al-Shafi'i's evidence by saying: If possession has two types, then disposal also has two types, because a person sometimes disposes for himself directly, and sometimes disposes as a representative of another, such as a deputy (wakil) and a mudarib (investment agent) who do not dispose for themselves but for the principal and the capital owner. So if, in not considering possession without disposal as proof of ownership, the possibility of trust possession is considered, then based on the possibility we mentioned (representative disposal), disposal will also not be proof of ownership. And if the possibility of representative disposal is not considered, then the possibility of trust possession will also not be considered. When the possibility of trust possession is not considered, then bare possession will be proof that the possessor is the owner, and it will be permissible to testify to the possessor's ownership merely by seeing possession.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the validity of testifying to ownership based solely on seeing possession (qabda). The Hanafi position, as stated in the matn of Imam Muhammad, is that it is permissible to testify that a person is the owner of an item if he is seen in possession of it, because possession is the ultimate outward indicator of ownership. The author provides a detailed proof (dalil) for this: all causes of ownership (purchase, gift, charity, inheritance) ultimately culminate in possession, so possession serves as the apparent evidence. However, this is subject to two conditions: (1) there is no disputant challenging the possession, and (2) the witness should not mention the reason for his testimony before the judge, as that might affect the verdict. Imam Abu Yusuf adds a further condition—that the witness must have certainty in his heart that the possessor is the owner, citing the hadith 'When you know it like the sun, then testify; otherwise, leave it.' The Hanafi scholars reconcile this by treating Imam Abu Yusuf's narration as an interpretation of Imam Muhammad's absolute ruling, so the condition of certainty is effectively agreed upon. The passage then presents Imam al-Shafi'i's objection: possession alone is ambiguous because it could be trust possession (amanah) rather than ownership possession; only possession coupled with disposal (tasarruf) indicates ownership. The Hanafi response, given by the author of al-Hidayah, uses a parity argument: just as possession can be of two types (trust vs. ownership), disposal can also be of two types (direct and representative, e.g., an agent or mudarib). So if the possibility of trust possession is considered sufficient to deny testimony, then the possibility of representative disposal should equally deny the evidentiary value of disposal. Since the law does not consider the latter possibility, it should not consider the former either, thus upholding the validity of testimony based on mere possession.

Key Terms
قبضہ (qabda): Possession of a physical object, which is taken as an outward sign of ownership.
تصرف (tasarruf): Legal disposal or use of property, such as selling, using, or giving away.
امانت (amanah): Trust possession, where one holds property without being the owner, e.g., as a bailee or agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what additional condition did Imam Abu Yusuf require for testifying based on possession?
A) The witness must see the possessor using the item (tasarruf).
B) The witness must have certainty or a strong impression in his heart that the possessor is the owner.
C) The witness must be a close relative of the possessor.
D) The witness must have seen the item for at least one year.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits testimony based on possession alone, provided the witness has genuine belief, and rebuts the Shafi'i objection that disposal is necessary through a parity argument about the types of disposal.

Conditions for Testifying After Seeing Owner and Property
States the ruling on testifying after seeing owner and property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three possible scenarios of visual knowledge that permit testimony about ownership in the Hanafi school and apply the rule of istihsan.

Core Idea
Testimony requires direct sight of both owner and property, but sight of property boundaries alone suffices by istihsan.

In Hanafi law, testimony about ownership is not left to mere assumption. The school sets clear conditions based on what the witness has personally seen. This passage from al-Hidayah lays down the basic rule and its istihsan exception, connecting property boundaries to lineage through the principle of common report (tasaamu').

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The text presents three possibilities: (1) seeing both owner and property, (2) seeing only the property with its boundaries, (3) seeing the owner without the property, or seeing neither. Classify each scenario.
Focus on the second scenario – the istihsan exception.
2
Apply the general rule
The default rule is that testimony is permissible only when the witness has physically seen both the owner and the property. This reflects the principle that testimony must be based on certain knowledge.
Certainty comes from direct sight (mu'āyana).
3
Understand the istihsan exception
If the witness saw the property with its defined boundaries but not the owner, testimony is still permissible by istihsan. The reason given: ownership of property, like lineage, can be established through widespread report (tasaamu').
Istihsan here is based on the ease of establishing boundaries through common knowledge.
4
Know when testimony is not allowed
If the witness saw neither the owner nor the property, or saw the owner without the property, testimony is not permissible. The absence of visual knowledge of the property itself makes the testimony defective.
Seeing the owner alone is insufficient because the object of ownership (the property) remains unknown.

Source Text

ثم المسألة على وجوه ان عاين المالك والملك حل له ان يشهد وكذا اذا عاين الملك بحدوده دون المالك استحسانا لان النسب يثبت بالتسامع فيحصل معرفته وان لم يعاينهما او عاين المالك دون
الملك لا يحل له
It is permissible to give testimony after seeing the owner and the property. Then the issue has several aspects: if he saw the owner and the property, it is permissible for him to testify. Similarly, if he saw the property with its boundaries without the owner, it is permissible by istihsan, because lineage is established by common report (tasaamu'), so its knowledge is obtained. But if he saw neither, or saw the owner without the property, it is not permissible for him.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This is a ruling statement that clarifies the conditions under which a witness may testify regarding ownership. The general rule is that testimony is permissible only if the witness has directly seen both the owner and the property (milk). However, the Hanafi school also permits testimony by istihsan if the witness has seen the property with its defined boundaries but not the owner, because ownership of property (like lineage) can be known through widespread reputation (tasaamu'). Conversely, if the witness has seen neither the owner nor the property, or has seen the owner but not the property, testimony is not allowed. This ruling summarizes the chapter's key principle.

Key Terms
معاینہ (mu'āyana): Direct visual observation or sighting of the owner or property.
تسامع (tasaamu'): Hearsay or common knowledge that spreads among people; used here to establish lineage and, by extension, ownership of property with known boundaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the witness saw the property with its boundaries.
B) If the witness heard the owner claim ownership.
C) If the witness saw the owner but not the property.
D) If the witness saw neither owner nor property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi testimony law, permissible testimony about ownership requires either sight of both owner and property, or sight of the property with defined boundaries by istihsan based on common report.

Testifying to Ownership: Possession vs. Certainty
Inter-madhhab dispute on testimony based on possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi condition for admitting testimony based on possession and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position using the qiyas of disposal types.

Core Idea
Possession alone never proves ownership; the witness must be certain that the possessor is the owner.

In this passage from the Book of Testimony, the Hidayah tackles a critical rule for witnesses: can you testify that someone owns an object just because you saw them holding it? The Hanafi school says no—because possession could be as a trustee. Even seeing them use it is not enough, because they could be acting as an agent. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi internal difference and the Shafi'i challenge.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling on testimony based on mere possession
The witness must have inner certainty (rajahan) that the possessor is the owner; mere possession does not suffice.
The text says 'jaa'iz nahi' (not permissible) for testifying without conviction.
2
Reconcile the internal Hanafi difference
Imam Muhammad's matn allowed testimony based on possession, but Imam Abu Yusuf's narration requires conviction. The mashayikh interpret the matn as requiring conviction.
This reconciliation is important for understanding the mufta bihi position.
3
Present the Shafi'i objection and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that possession with disposal (tasarruf) is sufficient evidence because disposal is an act of ownership.
Shafi'i sees disposal as a stronger indicator than mere possession.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response using the qiyas of two types of disposal
Hanafis counter that disposal can be by agency (e.g., wakeel, mudarib), so it does not prove ownership either.
The qiyas: just as possession can be trust or ownership, disposal can be for self or as deputy.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Shafi'i law, a witness may testify after seeing use; under Hanafi law, the witness needs deeper certainty.
This affects court outcomes dramatically.

Source Text

ا۔ امانت کا قبضہ
-۲ ملک کا قبضہ
یعنی جس طرح آدمی مالک ہونے کی وجہ سے قبضہ کرتا ہے اسی طرح امین ہونے کی وجہ سے قبضہ کرتا ہے پس نفس قبضہ، قابض کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ہوسکتا ہے کہ اس کا قبضہ مالکانہ نہ ہو بلکہ امانت کے طور پر ہو اور جب نفس قبضہ قابض کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل نہیں ہے تو محض قبضہ دیکھ کر قابض کے مالک ہونے کی گواہی دینا بھی جائز نہ ہوگا ہاں اگر قبضہ کے ساتھ قابض شی، مقبوض میں تصرف بھی کرتا ہے تو یہ قبضہ مع التصرف اس کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل ہوگا کیونکہ تصرف کا مجاز مالک ہوتا ہے نہ کہ امین پس ثابت ہو گیا کہ خالص قبضہ قابض کی ملکیت کی دلیل نہیں ہے بلکہ قبضہ مع التصرف اسکے مالک ہونے کی دلیل ہے۔

Book of Testimony. 56. Ashraf al-Hidayah volume - giving doubt is not permissible. From this it became clear that mere possession is not sufficient for testifying to ownership, rather it is necessary that a conviction arises in the witness's heart that the possessor is the owner. The author of Hidayah stated that the opinion of the Hanafi mashayikh is that the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf is an interpretation of the absolute narration of Imam Muhammad, i.e., the issue of the matn. That is, Imam Muhammad only said in the matn that if there is possession, then testifying to ownership is permissible. But it is possible that its meaning is that it comes into his heart that his possession is as an owner, then testifying is permissible. So if the meaning of Imam Muhammad's matn is this, then the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf becomes an interpretation of that absolute matn, and the coming of this certainty in the witness's heart becomes a condition according to all. Imam al-Shafi'i said that the evidence of ownership is the possession that is accompanied by disposal (tasarruf); that is, if the possessor disposes of the possessed item, then his possession is evidence of his ownership, and it is permissible for the witness to testify to possession with disposal and that the possessor is the owner. And if he does not dispose, then possession without disposal is not evidence of ownership, and it is not permissible for the witness to testify on the basis of that possession that the possessor is the owner. This is the saying of some of our Hanafi mashayikh. Because possession is of two types: 1. Possession of trust (amanah), 2. Possession of ownership (milk). That is, just as a person takes possession because he is an owner, similarly he takes possession because he is a trustee. So mere possession is not evidence that the possessor is the owner, because it is possible that his possession is not as an owner but as a trust. And when mere possession is not evidence that the possessor is the owner, then testifying based merely on seeing possession that the possessor is the owner is also not permissible. Yes, if along with possession the possessor also disposes of the possessed item, then this possession with disposal is evidence of his ownership, because the one authorized to dispose is the owner, not the trustee. So it is established that pure possession is not evidence of the possessor's ownership, but rather possession with disposal is evidence of his ownership. The author of Hidayah, while responding to Imam al-Shafi'i's argument, said that if possession is of two types, then disposal is also of two types, because sometimes a person disposes for himself, and sometimes he disposes as a deputy for another, like a wakeel and a mudarib do not dispose for themselves but dispose for the muwakkil and the rabb al-mal. So if in not considering possession without disposal as evidence of ownership, the probability of trust possession is considered, then on the basis of the probability we mentioned (agency in disposal), disposal also would not be evidence of ownership. And if the probability of agency in disposal is not considered, then the probability of trust possession also will not be considered, and when the probability of trust possession is not considered, then mere possession becomes evidence that the possessor is the owner, and testifying based merely on seeing possession that the possessor is the owner becomes permissible.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the evidentiary value of possession (qabd) for testifying to ownership. The discussion also includes an internal Hanafi reconciliation between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad. The core ruling: mere possession does not permit a witness to testify that the possessor is the owner, because possession can be by way of trust (amanah) as well as ownership. The witness must have a conviction (rajahan) that the possessor is the owner. Imam Abu Yusuf's view, according to the mashayikh, interprets Imam Muhammad's absolute statement in the matn—that possession allows testimony—as requiring this inner conviction. Imam al-Shafi'i argued that possession accompanied by disposal (tasarruf) is sufficient evidence. The Hanafi response: disposal can also be by agency (niyaba), as in the case of a wakeel or mudarib, so disposal alone does not prove ownership. The author uses a qiyas (analogy) between the two types of possession and the two types of disposal to show that neither is decisive. The conclusion is that the witness must have actual knowledge ('ilm) that the possessor is the owner; mere observation of possession or even disposal is not enough if it could be explained otherwise.

Key Terms
قَبْض (qabd): Text: 'possession' – taking physical control of an item. The passage distinguishes between possession as an owner (milk) and possession as a trustee (amanah).
تَصَرُّف (tasarruf): Text: 'disposal' – any act of using, selling, leasing, or otherwise dealing with property. The passage notes that tasarruf can be for oneself or on behalf of another.
أَمَانَة (amanah): Text: 'trust' – possession held without ownership, such as by a depositary or borrower.
شَهَادَة (shahadah): Text: 'testimony' – giving evidence in court regarding ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is possession with disposal (qabd ma‘a al-tasarruf) not sufficient for a witness to testify to ownership?
A) Because disposal requires a formal contract to be reliable.
B) Because disposal can be done as a trustee or agent, not only as an owner.
C) Because the witness must have seen the original purchase.
D) Because possession without disposal is always stronger evidence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school insists on the witness's inner certainty of ownership; neither possession nor disposal alone suffices because both can arise from non-ownership bases.

Four Scenarios of Witnessing Owner and Property
Parsing four scenarios of testimony validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios of witnessing owner and property for valid testimony and identify the scenario where istihsan permits testimony despite lack of direct observation of the owner.

Core Idea
Testimony requires direct knowledge of the property; hearsay suffices for the owner's identity by istihsan.

This passage from Al-Hidayah is a classic parsing exercise on the conditions for valid testimony. It enumerates four possible scenarios regarding the witness's direct observation (mu'ayanah) of the owner and the property (mamluk). The ruling turns on whether the witness has certain knowledge of both elements. An important exception by istihsan allows testimony when the property is seen but the owner is known only by hearsay, because lineage can be established by common report.

Guided Path
1
List the four scenarios
Identify the four combinations of witnessing: (1) both owner and property seen, (2) neither seen, (3) property seen but not owner, (4) owner seen but not property.
The term 'property' (milk) here means the owned object (mamluk).
2
Apply the default rule
The default rule is that testimony is valid only if the witness has direct observation (mu'ayanah) of both the owner and the property. This is because the witness must have certain knowledge ('ilm) of what he testifies.
3
Recognize the istihsan exception
In scenario (3) – property seen but owner not seen – qiyas would reject the testimony because the owner is unknown. However, istihsan permits it because the owner's lineage (nasab) can be established through hearsay (tasammu')
The exception rests on the principle that for lineage, hearsay is sufficient.
4
Determine the ruling for each scenario
Scenarios (1) and (3) by istihsan: permissible (ja'iz). Scenarios (2) and (4): not permissible (ghayr ja'iz).
In scenario (4), seeing the owner alone is insufficient because the property itself is unknown.
5
Justify the istihsan
The commentary explains that the owner's identity becomes known through common report, making the mashhud lahu (person for whom testimony is given) known, and thus testimony is valid.
This is a distinctive Hanafi position.

ملک ( مملوک ) کا معائنہ کیا اور مالک کا نہ کیا.
-۴- مالک کا معائنہ کیا ہوا اور ملک کا معائنہ نہ کیا ہو۔
ان چاروں صورتوں میں ملک سے مراد مملوک ہے۔ پس اگر پہلی صورت ہے یعنی کسی نے مالک کا معائنہ کیا ہے اس طور پر کہ اس نے مالک کو اسکے نام ، نسب اور شکل کے ساتھ پہچان لیا اور ملک کا بھی معائنہ کر لیا ہے اس طور پر کہ اس نے شی مملوک کو اسکے حدود اور حقوق کے ساتھ پہچان لیا اور اس مٹی مملوک کو بغیر کسی جھگڑے کے اس کے قبضہ میں بھی دیکھا ہے اور اس دیکھنے والے کے دل میں یہ بات بھی اتر گئی کہ یہ فیٹی اس قابض کی ہے تو معائنہ اور مشاہدہ کر نیوالے آدمی کو یہ گواہی دینا جائز ہے کہ پیشی ، قابض کی ملک ہے اور قابض ہی اس شی کا مالک ہے کیونکہ گواہ نے علم اور بصیرت کیسا تھ گواہی دی ہے اور سابق میں گزر چکا ہے کہ جو گواہی علم اور بصیرت کے ساتھ ہو اس کو قبول کیا جاتا ہے لہذا اس صورت میں بھی گواہ کی گواہی قبول کے قابل ہوگی ۔ اور اگر دوسری صورت ہے یعنی گواہ نے مالک اور ملک دونوں کا معائنہ نہیں کیا بلکہ لوگوں سے سنا ہے کہ فلاں شہر میں فلاں کی اس قدر جائیداد ہے حالانکہ گواہ نہ اس جائیداد کو پہچانتا ہے نہ اسپر کسی کا قبضہ دیکھا ہے تو گواہ کو یہ گواہی دینا جائز ہے کہ یہ جائیداد فلاں بن فلاں کی ملکیت ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں گواہ کی گواہی محض انکل اور اندازے سے ہے حالانکہ ادائے شہادت کیلئے شہود بہ کا علم ضروری ہے پس ادائے شہادت کے لئے شہود بہ کا علم شرط ہے اور یہاں علم پایا نہیں گیا تو اس صورت میں گواہی دینا بھی جائز نہ ہوگا اور تیسری صورت یعنی گواہ نے ملک کا معائنہ تو کیا ہے لیکن نہ تو مالک کی شکل سے آشنا ہے اور نہ اس کے نسب سے واقف ہے بلکہ محض سننے کی بنیاد پر یہ کہتا ہے کہ فلاں جائیداد جس کا میں نے معائنہ کیا ہے وہ فلاں بن فلاں کی طرف منسوب ہے تو اس صورت میں قیاس کا نقاضہ یہ ہے کہ اس کے لئے فلاں بن فلاں کی ملکیت کی گواہی دینا جائز نہ ہو کیونکہ یہ مشہودلہ (جسکے لئے گواہی دی گئی ) کے مجہول ہونے کے باوجود مالک کے لئے ملک کی گواہی دینا ہے اور یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ مشہور بہ کی جہالت جو از شہادت کے لئے مانع ہے۔ لہذا مشہودلہ کی جہالت بھی جواز شہادت کیلئے مانع ہوگی ، ہاں استحسانا گواہی دینا جائز ہے اس لئے کہ اس صورت میں ملک تو مشاہدہ سے معلوم ہوگئی اور مالک کا نسب عام لوگوں سے سنکر ثابت ہو گیا کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ نسب کی گواہی دینے کیلئے سماع وسٹا ، کافی ہے پس اس صورت میں بھی معلوم چیز کی گواہی معلوم شخص کے لئے ہوئی یعنی مشہور بہ اور مشہو دلۂ دونوں معلوم ہوئے اور ان دونوں
After witnessing the owner and the property, testifying is permissible. Then the issue has aspects: if he directly observed the owner and the property, it is permissible for him to testify. Likewise, if he observed the property with its boundaries without the owner, [it is permissible] by istihsan, because lineage (nasab) is established by hearsay (tasammu‘), so his knowledge is obtained. But if he observed neither, or observed the owner without the property, it is not permissible for him. [Page 57] Book of Shahada. Ashraf al-Hidayah volume: same translation. Then the issue has several forms. If he observed both the owner and the property, then testifying is permissible. Similarly, if he observed the property with its boundaries and did not see the owner, then also by istihsan (it is permissible to testify), because the lineage of the owner becomes known by hearing from people, so his identification is obtained. And if he did not observe both the owner and the property, or he observed the owner but not the property, then for him it is not permissible to give testimony. Commentary: The author of Hidayah said that this issue has four forms: 1. The witness observed both the owner and the property (the owned thing). 2. He observed neither. 3. He observed the property (owned thing) but not the owner. 4. He observed the owner but not the property. In all four forms, 'property' means the owned thing. So if the first form—someone observed the owner in a way that he recognized the owner by his name, lineage, and appearance, and also observed the property in a way that he recognized the owned thing by its boundaries and rights, and saw that owned thing in his possession without any dispute, and in the observer's heart it also settled that this thing belongs to this possessor—then for the person who did the observation, it is permissible to testify that this thing is the property of the possessor and that the possessor is the owner of this thing, because the witness has given testimony with knowledge and insight, and it has passed earlier that testimony given with knowledge and insight is accepted. So in this case, the witness's testimony will be worthy of acceptance. If the second form—the witness did not observe both owner and property, but heard from people that in such-and-such city, so-and-so has such-and-such property, while the witness neither recognizes that property nor has seen anyone in possession of it—then it is not permissible for the witness to testify that this property belongs to so-and-so, because in this form the witness's testimony is based merely on conjecture and estimation, whereas for performance of testimony, knowledge of the subject matter (mashhud bihi) is necessary. So for performance of testimony, knowledge of the subject matter is a condition, and here knowledge is not found, so it is not permissible for him to testify. The third form—the witness observed the property but is neither familiar with the owner's appearance nor his lineage, but on the basis of mere hearing says, 'The property I observed is attributed to so-and-so'—then the requirement of qiyas is that it is not permissible for him to testify to the ownership of so-and-so, because this is testifying to property for an unknown person (mashhud lahu) despite his being unknown, and it is agreed that ignorance of the person for whom testimony is given is a preventer of the validity of testimony. So ignorance of the mashhud lahu is also a preventer of the validity of testimony. However, by istihsan, testifying is permissible, because in this case the property became known by observation, and the owner's lineage is established by hearing from common people, since it has passed earlier that for testifying to lineage, hearing (sama') [alone] is sufficient. So in this case, testimony of a known thing for a known person occurred, i.e., both the subject matter (mashhud bihi) and the person for whom testimony is given (mashhud lahu) became known.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement from the Hidayah matn, parsed and expanded by the commentary. It presents four scenarios regarding a witness's direct observation of the owner and the property (mamluk). The ruling: Testimony is valid only if the witness has direct knowledge ('ilm) of both the owner and the property. If he observes both, it is permissible. If he observes only the property with its boundaries, but not the owner, it is permissible by istihsan, because the owner's lineage (identity) can be established through common report (tasam'u). If he observes neither, or observes the owner but not the property, it is not permissible. The commentary details each scenario: (1) Witness knows the owner by name, lineage, appearance, and the property with its boundaries and possession; testimony is valid (with knowledge). (2) No observation at all—mere hearsay; invalid because no knowledge. (3) Property seen but owner known only by hearsay; qiyas would reject it because the owner is unknown, but istihsan accepts it because lineage can be proven by hearsay, and thus both object and person become known. (4) Owner seen but property not seen; invalid because the property itself is unknown.

Key Terms
مُعَايَنَة (mu‘āyanah): Direct observation – seeing with one's own eyes, as opposed to hearsay. It is the primary method of acquiring knowledge for testimony.
اِسْتِحْسَان (istihsān): Juristic preference – setting aside qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling based on maslahah or custom.
تَسَامُع (tasāmu‘): Hearsay – information received by listening to common talk. In testimony, hearsay is accepted for establishing lineage (nasab) as a basis for identifying the owner.
مَشْهُود لَهُ (mashhud lahu): The person in whose favor testimony is given – the owner whose ownership is being testified. The passage emphasizes that this person must be known for testimony to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which scenario does the Hanafi school accept testimony by istihsan, contrary to qiyas?
A) When the witness sees both the owner and the property.
B) When the witness sees neither the owner nor the property.
C) When the witness sees the property but not the owner, and knows the owner by hearsay.
D) When the witness sees the owner but not the property.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is valid only when the witness has direct knowledge of the property; knowledge of the owner may be acquired by hearsay due to istihsan.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Four Scenarios for Testimony Based on Observation of Owner and Property
Four-scenario analysis of testimony validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four scenarios for testimony based on observation of owner and property and state the ruling for each scenario according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Testimony requires direct knowledge—except when istihsan allows hearsay for lineage.

In the Hanafi school, testimony about ownership is not simply about what you saw; it depends on whether you have certain knowledge of both the owner and the property. This passage from al-Hidayah outlines four scenarios that test the boundaries of direct observation and hearsay. Understanding these scenarios sharpens your ability to evaluate the validity of testimony in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
List the four combinations of observing/not observing the owner and the property. The passage defines them clearly: (1) both observed, (2) neither observed, (3) property observed but not owner, (4) owner observed but not property.
Property here refers to the owned thing (mamluk), not the abstract concept.
2
Apply the requirement of knowledge (ilm)
For each scenario, determine whether the witness has certain knowledge of the subject of testimony (mashhud bih) and the person for whom testimony is given (mashhud lahu). The general rule is that testimony must be based on knowledge, not conjecture.
Knowledge can be direct (by sight) or indirect (by reliable hearsay for lineage).
3
Apply the rule that testimony requires knowledge
Scenario 1 clearly satisfies both types of knowledge. Scenario 2 lacks all knowledge. Scenario 4 lacks knowledge of the property. Scenario 3 lacks direct knowledge of the owner but has knowledge of the property.
4
Analyze the role of istihsan in scenario 3
By strict analogy (qiyas), scenario 3 would also be invalid because the owner (mashhud lahu) is unknown. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), testimony is allowed because the owner's lineage can be reliably known through widespread hearsay (istirad), which is accepted as sufficient for knowledge of lineage.
This exception applies only when the hearsay is widespread (not just one person).
5
Conclude the fourth scenario
Scenario 4 is invalid because the property itself (mashhud bih) is unknown, and there is no istihsan to override this. The witness may know the owner but cannot testify to ownership of something they have not seen.
This shows that knowledge of the property is a non-negotiable condition.

Source Text

كتاب الشهادة
اشرف الہدا یہ جلد۔ وہم ترجمہ پھر مسئلہ کی کئی صورتیں ہیں۔ اگر اس نے مالک اور ملک دونوں کا مشاہدہ کیا ہے تو اس لئے گواہی دینا جائز ہے اور اسی طرح اگر اس نے ملک کو اسکے حدود کے ساتھ مشاہدہ کیا ہو اور مالک کو نہ دیکھا ہوگا تو بھی استحسانا ( گواہی دینا حلال ہے) کیونکہ مالک کا نسب لوگوں سے سنکر ہو جاتا ہے لہذا اس کی شناخت حاصل ہو جائیگی۔ اور اگر اس نے مالک اور ملک دونوں کا مشاہدہ نہ کیا ہو یا مالک کا مشاہدہ کیا ہو اور
ملک کا نہ کیا ہو تو اس کیلئے گواہی دنیا حلال نہیں ہے۔
تشریح صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا کہ اس مسئلہ کی چار صورتیں ہیں،
-1 گواہ نے مالک اور ملک ( مملوک ) دونوں کا مشاہدہ اور معائنہ کیا ہو۔

مالک کے لئے ملک کی گواہی دینا ہے اور یہ بات مسلم ہے کہ مشہور بہ کی جہالت جو از شہادت کے لئے مانع ہے۔ لہذا مشہودلہ کی جہالت بھی جواز شہادت کیلئے مانع ہوگی ، ہاں استحسانا گواہی دینا جائز ہے اس لئے کہ اس صورت میں ملک تو مشاہدہ سے معلوم ہوگئی اور مالک کا نسب عام لوگوں سے سنکر ثابت ہو گیا کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ نسب کی گواہی دینے کیلئے سماع وسٹا ، کافی ہے پس اس صورت میں بھی معلوم چیز کی گواہی معلوم شخص کے لئے ہوئی یعنی مشہور بہ اور مشہو دلۂ دونوں معلوم ہوئے اور ان دونوں کے معلوم ہونے کی صورت میں گواہی دینا چونکہ جائز ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس صورت میں گواہی دینا استحسانا جائز ہوگا۔ اور چوتھی صورت یعنی گواہ نے مالک کا معائنہ تو کیا ہے لیکن ملک کا معائنہ نہیں کیا ہے تو اس صورت میں بھی گواہ کیلئے گواہی دینا جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ ملک ( مملوک ) کا معائنہ نہ کرنے کی وجہ سے مشہور بہ معلوم نہیں ہوا حالانکہ ادائے شہادت کے لئے مشہور بہ کا پورے طور پر معلوم ہونا شرط ہے پس جب ادائے شہادت کے لئے مشہور بہ کا معلوم ہونا شرط ہے اور اس چوتھی صورت میں مشہود یہ معلوم نہیں ہے تو اس صورت میں گواہ کے لئے گواہی دینا بھی جائز نہیں ہے۔
Book of Testimony. Ashraf al-Hidayah, volume 10. Translation: Then there are several forms of the issue. If he observed both the owner and the property, then giving testimony is permissible. Similarly, if he observed the property with its boundaries but did not see the owner, it is also permissible by istihsan (giving testimony is halal) because the lineage of the owner is known by hearing from people, so his identification is obtained. And if he observed neither the owner nor the property, or observed the owner but not the property, then giving testimony is not halal for him. Commentary: The author of al-Hidayah said that this issue has four forms: 1. The witness observed and inspected both the owner and the owned property. 2. He inspected neither. 3. He inspected the owned property but not the owner. 4. He inspected the owner but not the owned property. In all four forms, 'property' means the owned thing. So if it is the first form, i.e., someone inspected the owner in such a way that he recognized the owner by his name, lineage, and appearance, and also inspected the owned property in such a way that he recognized the owned thing by its boundaries and attributes, and saw that owned thing in his possession without any dispute, and it settled in his heart that this thing belongs to this possessor, then it is permissible for the person who inspected and observed to give testimony that the thing is the property of the possessor and the possessor is the owner of that thing, because the witness gave testimony with knowledge and insight, and it has passed earlier that testimony given with knowledge and insight is accepted. So in this case, the witness's testimony is worthy of acceptance. If it is the second form, i.e., the witness did not inspect either the owner or the property, but heard from people that so-and-so in such-and-such city has such-and-such property, while the witness neither recognizes that property nor saw anyone's possession over it, then it is not permissible for the witness to give testimony that this property belongs to so-and-so son of so-and-so, because in this form the witness's testimony is based on mere guess and estimate, whereas for giving testimony, knowledge of the subject of testimony is necessary. So knowledge of the subject of testimony is a condition for giving testimony, and here knowledge is not found, so giving testimony is not permissible. And the third form, i.e., the witness inspected the property but is neither familiar with the owner's appearance nor aware of his lineage, but on the basis of mere hearsay says that such-and-such property which I inspected is attributed to so-and-so son of so-and-so, then the requirement of analogy is that it is not permissible for him to give testimony of ownership for so-and-so son of so-and-so because this is giving testimony of ownership for the owner despite the person for whom testimony is given being unknown, and it is established that ignorance of the subject of testimony is a hindrance for the validity of testimony. Therefore ignorance of the person for whom testimony is given is also a hindrance for the permissibility of testimony. However, by istihsan, giving testimony is permissible because in this form the property is known through observation, and the owner's lineage is established by hearing from common people, as it has passed earlier that for giving testimony of lineage, hearing is sufficient. So in this form, testimony of a known thing is given for a known person, i.e., both the subject of testimony and the person for whom testimony is given are known, and when both are known, giving testimony is permissible. Therefore in this form, giving testimony by istihsan is permissible. And the fourth form, i.e., the witness inspected the owner but did not inspect the property, then in this form also it is not permissible for the witness to give testimony because due to not inspecting the property, the subject of testimony is unknown, whereas for giving testimony, complete knowledge of the subject of testimony is a condition. So when knowledge of the subject of testimony is a condition for giving testimony, and in this fourth form the subject of testimony is unknown, then in this form it is not permissible for the witness to give testimony.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah discusses the conditions for a witness to testify about someone's ownership of property based on direct observation. The author presents four scenarios: (1) The witness has observed both the owner and the property in detail, including recognizing the owner by name, lineage, and appearance, and the property by its boundaries and seeing it in the possessor's possession without dispute. In this case, testimony is permissible because the witness has direct knowledge. (2) The witness has observed neither the owner nor the property, but only heard rumors. This testimony is invalid because it lacks the required certain knowledge. (3) The witness has observed the property but not the owner, only knowing the owner's name by hearsay. By analogy, testimony would be invalid because the person for whom testimony is given (mashhud lahu) is unknown. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), testimony is allowed because the property is known by sight and the owner's lineage can be established through widespread reputation (istirad), as is accepted for lineage testimony. (4) The witness has observed the owner but not the property. Testimony is invalid because the property (mashhud bih) is unknown, which is a condition for valid testimony. The reasoning distinguishes between knowledge of the owner and knowledge of the property: both are required for valid testimony, but the means of knowledge may differ (direct sight vs. reliable hearsay for lineage).

Key Terms
مالک (malik): The owner of the property; the person whose ownership is being testified.
ملک (milk): The property or owned thing (mamluk) that is the subject of testimony.
مشاہدہ (mushahadah): Direct observation or seeing with one's own eyes.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides strict analogy for a more equitable ruling.
مشہود بہ (mashhud bihi): The subject of testimony, i.e., the property or thing being testified about.
مشہودلہ (mashhud lahu): The person for whom testimony is given, i.e., the claimed owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following scenarios is testimony regarding ownership permissible according to Hanafi jurisprudence as explained in this passage?
A) The witness observed the property but only heard the owner's name from an unreliable person.
B) The witness observed both the owner and the property in detail.
C) The witness observed neither the owner nor the property but heard from a crowd.
D) The witness observed the owner but not the property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi testimony law, direct observation of property and owner is ideal, but istihsan permits reliance on widespread hearsay for the owner's identity when the property is directly observed.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Testifying About Slave Ownership from Possession
Explains when a witness can testify about ownership of slaves observed in someone's possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a witness may testify about ownership of a slave based on possession, and apply the Hanafi qiyas and istihsan reasoning.

Core Idea
Possession indicates ownership — but only if the slave cannot assert control himself.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario in testimony (shahadah): when can a witness who sees a person in possession of slaves testify that the possessor is the owner? The ruling depends on whether the witness knows the person is a slave, and if not, whether the slave is a minor or an adult. The analysis involves analogical reasoning (qiyas) to inanimate property and an exceptional narration from Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage distinguishes three cases: (1) witness knows the persons are slaves, (2) witness does not know but they are minors, (3) witness does not know and they are adults.
Each scenario has a different ruling on permissibility of testimony.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For scenarios 1 and 2, testimony is permissible because the slave has no yad (control) over himself. For scenario 3, by qiyas, testimony is not permissible because the adult slave can assert control, removing the presumption of ownership. However, Abu Hanifa narrated an exception allowing testimony by analogy to clothes.
The main position excludes scenario 3.
3
Understand the illa (effective cause)
The illa for permitting testimony is that possession indicates ownership when the possessed item cannot assert autonomy. Slaves (like clothes) lack yad, but adult slaves can speak for themselves, breaking the analogy.
This is a khafiyyah (hidden) illa extracted by tanqih al-manat.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa's narration treats adult slaves like clothes, allowing testimony based purely on possession, overriding the autonomy objection. The school's relied-upon position prefers the distinction based on knowledge and age.
The text notes a difference (al-farq) between slaves and clothes, which is why the exception is not universally adopted.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
The operative position: testimony is valid if witness knows the persons are slaves (any age) or if they are minors. If witness does not know and they are adults, testimony is invalid, except according to Abu Hanifa's narration (weaker).
In practice, the mufta bihi follows the majority Hanafi view.

When can testimony be given about a male slave or a female slave? And as for the male slave and female slave: if he knows that they are slaves, then the same ruling applies because a slave does not have control over himself. And if he does not know that they are slaves, but they are minors who cannot express themselves, then the same ruling applies because they have no authority. And if they are adults, then this is the place of exception because they have authority over themselves, so they can repel the control of others over themselves, so the evidence of ownership is absent. And from Abu Hanifa (it is narrated) that it is permissible for him to testify regarding them as well, by analogy to clothes, and the difference is what we explained. And Allah knows best. Translation: And for the male slave and female slave: if a person recognizes that both are slaves, then the same ruling applies because a slave is not under his own control. And if he does not know that both are slaves, but they are minors who cannot express themselves, then also the same ruling because they have no authority. And if both are adults, then this is the place of exception because both have control over themselves; therefore, their having control over themselves will repel the control of others over them, so the evidence of ownership is absent. And from Abu Hanifa it is narrated that for him it is permissible to testify regarding them as well, by analogy to clothes, and the difference is what we have explained. And Allah knows best. Commentary: The explanation of the issue is that if a person sees a male slave or female slave in someone's possession serving him, then there are two scenarios: either he recognizes that they are slaves or not. If the observing person recognizes that they are slaves, then it is permissible for him to give testimony that the possessor is their owner, whether the slaves are minors (non-adult) or adults (sane), because with the attribute of slavery they have no control over themselves, and the observer saw them in another's possession, so this is like seeing cloth in another's possession, and it has passed earlier that if someone sees cloth or any other thing in someone's possession, it is permissible for the observer to give testimony of the possessor's ownership. So similarly, seeing a slave in someone's possession, it is permissible to give testimony of the possessor's ownership, provided the observer (witness) recognizes that they are slaves. And if the observer does not recognize that they are slaves, then there are two scenarios: either the slaves are such minors who cannot express their inner thoughts, or they are adults, i.e., sensible enough to express their inner thoughts. So if they are such unintelligent children who cannot express their inner thoughts, then in this case also it is permissible for the observer to give testimony that the possessor is their owner, because in this case also they have no control over themselves, and the observer saw them in another's possession, so this is like seeing cloth or any other item in another's possession, and cloth...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of testifying about the ownership of slaves based on seeing them in someone's possession. The author distinguishes two main situations: when the witness knows the persons are slaves, and when he does not. If the witness knows they are slaves, testimony is always permissible, regardless of whether the slaves are minors or adults, because slaves have no legal control (yad) over themselves, analogously to inanimate property like clothes. If the witness does not know they are slaves, then the ruling depends on whether the slaves are minors (saqeer) who cannot speak for themselves, in which case testimony is still allowed because they lack control; or adults (kabeer) who are capable, in which case by strict analogy (qiyas) testimony would not be allowed because they can assert their own control, removing the presumption of the possessor's ownership. However, Imam Abu Hanifa reportedly allowed testimony in the adult case as well, by analogy to clothes, meaning that possession still gives a strong indication of ownership. The text notes a difference (between slaves and clothes) that was previously explained, possibly relating to the fact that adults can speak and claim ownership themselves. The relied-upon position seems to be the exception for adults, so testimony is allowed only if the witness knows they are slaves or if they are minors; otherwise, it is not allowed except according to Abu Hanifa's narration.

Key Terms
رقیق (raqiq): Slave, i.e., a person who is legally owned by another.
ید (yad): Legal control or authority; a slave is said to have no yad over himself.
صغیر (saghir): Minor, non-adult; someone who cannot legally express consent or assert control.
کبیر (kabir): Adult, sane person who can speak and act independently.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to derive rulings for new cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, because possession indicates ownership.
B) Never, because the adult slave has control over himself.
C) It is permissible only according to one narration from Abu Hanifa by analogy to clothes.
D) Only if the slave is a minor.

Key Takeaway
A witness may testify to ownership of a slave based on possession only if he knows the person is a slave, or if the slave is a minor; for an adult slave, testimony is not allowed by qiyas, but Abu Hanifa permitted it by analogy to clothes.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate

Testimony on Slave Ownership Based on Possession: The Exception for Rational Slaves
Ruling on testimony based on possession of slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that a witness cannot testify to ownership of a rational adult slave based solely on possession, and distinguish when possession is sufficient.

Core Idea
Possession of a rational slave is not evidence of ownership because the slave's own autonomous control defeats the presumption.

This lesson examines a key ruling in the Book of Testimony: when is witnessing a slave in someone's possession sufficient to testify to that person's ownership? The general rule is that possession indicates ownership, but an exception exists when the slave is a rational adult. We will explore the reasoning behind this exception, the conditions that trigger it, and a disagreement within the Hanafi school between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving testimony about the ownership of a slave based on seeing them in someone's possession. Determine the hukm (permissible or not) for different scenarios.
2
Understand the general rule
If the witness knows the slave is a slave, testimony is permissible regardless of the slave's age, because slavery removes autonomous control (yad).
3
Identify the exception
If the witness does not know the slave's status and the slave is a rational adult, testimony is not permissible. The slave's yad over himself defeats the evidence of possession.
This is the relied-upon position (Sahibayn).
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that even for rational adults, testimony is permissible by analogy with clothing. Understand the proof and the rebuttal based on the difference (yad vs. no yad).
The mufta bihi is the exception view.

ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں بھی ان کو اپنی ذات پر قابو حاصل نہیں ہے۔ اور دیکھنے والے نے ان کو دوسرے کے قبضہ میں دیکھا ہے تو یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کسی نے دوسرے کے قبضہ میں کپڑا وغیرہ کوئی سامان دیکھا ہو اور کپڑا وغیر سامان کسی کے قبضہ میں دیکھ کر چونکہ قابض کے مالک ہونے کی گواہی دینا جائز ہے اس لئے مذکورہ صورت میں بھی گواہی و بینا جائز ہے کہ قابض اس غلام اور باندی کا مالک ہے اور اگر وہ سمجھدار ہوں خواہ بالغ ہوں یا غیر بالغ ہوں تو یہی صورت استثناء (سوی العبد و الامة ) کا محل ہے یعنی اس صورت میں محض کسی کا قبضہ دیکھر یہ گواہی دینا جائز نہیں ہے کہ قابض اس غلام یا با ندی کا مالک ہے ۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں غلام اور باندی کو اپنی ذات پر قابو حاصل ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر وہ اپنے پیدائشی آزاد ہونے کا دعوی کر دے تو اسی کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور اس پر کسی کا قبضہ ثابت نہ ہوگا پس جب ان کو اپنی ذات پر قابو حاصل ہے تو یہ ان دونوں سے دوسرے کا قبضہ دور کر دیگا اور جب ان سے دوسرے کا قبضہ دور ہو گیا تو ملکیت کی دلیل (یعنی قبضہ ) معدوم ہوگئی اور جب ملکیت کی دلیل معدوم ہو گئی تو دیکھنے والے کے لئے قابض کے مالک ہونے کی گواہی دینا بھی جائز نہ ہوگا۔ حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ سے روایت ہے کہ اگر وہ غلام اور باندی کبیر یعنی سمجھدار ہوں تو بھی دیکھنے والے کو قابض کے مالک ہونے کی گواہی دینا حلال اور جائز ہے اس روایت کی دلیل کپڑوں اور دیگر سامانوں پر قیاس ہے یعنی جس طرح کسی کے قبضہ میں کپڑے دیکھ کر قابض کے مالک ہونے کی گواہی دینا جائز ہے اگر چہ غلام اور باندی کبیر ہوں یعنی سمجھ دار ہوں جو اپنے مافی الضمیر کو اداء کر سکتے ہوں یہی صاحبین سے مروی ہے گویا تینوں حضرات نے مذکورہ تمام صورتوں میں قبضہ کو ملکیت کی دلیل قرار دیا ہے چنانچہ اگر کسی نے یہ دعوی کیا کہ حامد کے قبضہ میں جو غلام یا باندی ہے میں اس کا مالک ہوں اور قابض اپنے مالک ہونے کا دعویٰ کرتا ہے تو قابض کا ہی قول قبول ہوگا ۔ کیونکہ قبضہ کی وجہ سے ظاہر حال اسی کے موافق ہے لیکن فرق وہ ہے جو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں یعنی کپڑوں کا اپنے اور ذاتی اختیار نہیں ہوتا ہے لہذا ان پر قابض کا قبضہ معتبر ہو گا اور سمجھ دار غلام اور باندی کا چونکہ اپنے اوپر ذاتی اختیار ہوتا ہے اس لئے ان پر کسی کا قبضہ معتبر نہ ہوگا۔ پس جب کپڑوں میں قابض کا قبضہ معتبر ہے تو اس کا قبضہ دلیل ملک ہو گا اور سمجھ دار غلام اور باندی میں چونکہ کسی کا قبضہ معتبر نہیں ہے اس لئے قابض کا ظاہری قبضہ اس کے مالک ہونے کی دلیل نہ ہوگا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب ! جمیل احمد عفی عنہ
When can testimony be given about a slave and slave-girl? As for the male slave and female slave: if he knows that both are slaves, then similarly because a slave does not have autonomous control (yad) over himself. And if he does not know that both are slaves, but they are minors who cannot express themselves, then similarly because they have no autonomous control. And if they are adult (rational), then that is the exception because they have autonomous control over themselves, which would negate the control of another over them, so the evidence of ownership is absent. And it is reported from Abu Hanifa that it is permissible for him to testify in that case as well, drawing an analogy with clothing. And the difference is what we have explained. And Allah knows best. Translation and commentary follow in Urdu: The issue is that if a person sees a slave or slave-girl in someone's possession serving him, there are two possibilities: either he knows they are slaves or not. If he knows they are slaves, it is permissible to testify that the possessor is the owner, whether they are minor or adult, because slavery gives them no control. If he does not know they are slaves, and they are minors unable to express themselves, it is also permissible by analogy to clothing. If they are rational adults (whether baligh or not), this is the exception: mere possession is not sufficient for testimony, because they have control over themselves and could claim freedom. This is the view of the Sahibayn. However, it is reported from Imam Abu Hanifa that it is permissible even for rational adults, by analogy with clothing. The practical difference is that in a dispute, the possessor is believed due to apparent state, but for rational slaves, possession is not reliable evidence of ownership.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Testimony addresses when witnessing a slave in someone's possession is sufficient to testify to that person's ownership. It is a ruling with proof, using reasoning (istihsan and qiyas). The matn states the general rule: if the witness knows the individuals are slaves, mere possession indicates ownership, because slaves lack autonomous control (yad). The same applies if the witness does not know their status but they are minors unable to speak for themselves. However, if they are rational adults (kabir), this is an exception: possession alone is not enough, because they can assert their own freedom, thereby defeating the presumption of ownership. The commentator then presents a riwaya from Abu Hanifa that even in this case, testimony is permissible by analogy with clothing. The relied-upon mufta bihi position is the exception (the view of the Sahibayn). The commentary explains the scenarios in detail: if the witness knows the slaves are slaves, testimony is permissible regardless of their age; if they are unknown and irrational, testimony is also permissible; if they are unknown and rational, testimony is not permissible based on possession alone, because they have yad over themselves. Abu Hanifa's view is based on qiyas, but the difference is that clothing has no will, while a rational slave does.

Key Terms
ید (yad): Autonomous control or authority; in this context, the legal capacity of a person to have dominion over themselves or property.
قبضہ (qabda): Physical possession; in fiqh, it is a primary indicator of ownership in cases of testimony.
استثناء (istithna'): Exception; in this ruling, the case where testimony is not allowed based on possession alone for rational slaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi), is testimony permissible if a witness sees a rational adult slave in someone's possession without knowing the slave's status?
A) Yes, because possession is always evidence of ownership.
B) No, because the slave has autonomous control (yad) over himself and could claim freedom.
C) Yes, by analogy with clothing.
D) No, but only if the slave is a minor.

Key Takeaway
Possession alone is sufficient evidence of ownership only when the possessed entity lacks autonomous control; rational slaves are excepted because their own yad defeats the presumption.

The Logical Order of Conditions in Testimony
Chapter heading and rationale for order of topics

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi jurists discuss the subject matter (mahall) of testimony before the witnesses, using the principle that conditions precede the conditioned.

Core Idea
Conditions logically precede the conditioned – the subject matter is a condition for testimony.

In this introductory passage, the commentator explains why the previous section on admissible subject matters came before this chapter on witnesses. The reasoning is that the thing testified about is a condition (shart) for testimony, and conditions must be established before the conditioned. This logical ordering reflects the systematic method of Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the text distinguishes between 'things' (subject matters) and 'people' (witnesses). The subject matter is called mahall (locus).
Mahall is the thing about which testimony is given.
2
Understand the relationship
The passage states that the mahall is a condition (shart) for testimony. Without an admissible subject matter, testimony cannot proceed.
This is a logical, not temporal, priority.
3
Apply the rule of priority
Conditions logically precede the conditioned. Therefore, the discussion of conditions is placed before the discussion of the conditioned.
This is a standard Hanafi usul principle.
4
Extract the rationale
The commentator uses this logical ordering to justify why the section on what can be testified to came before who can testify.
This shows the careful structuring of fiqh texts.

باب من يقبل شهادته ومن لا يقبل
ترجمہ یہ باب ان لوگوں کے بیان میں ہے جنکی گواہی قبول کی جاتی ہے اور ان لوگوں کے بیان میں ہے جنکی گواہی قبول نہیں کی جاتی ہے۔ تشریح گزشتہ فصل میں ان چیزوں کا بیان تھا جس میں شہادت سنی جاتی ہے اور ان چیزوں کا بیان تھا جس میں شہادت نہیں سنی جاتی ۔ اس سے فراغت کے بعد اس باب میں ان لوگوں کا بیان کیا جائیگا جن کی شہادت قبول کی جاتی ہے اور جن کی شہادت قبول نہیں کی جاتی مگر چونکہ وہ چیزیں جن میں شہادت قبول کی جاتی ہے یا قبول نہیں کی جاتی شہادت کا محل ہیں اور محل شرط ہوتے ہیں۔ اور شرط ، مشروط پر مقدم ہوتی ہیں اس لئے ان چیزوں کا بیان جن کی شہادت قبول کی جاتی ہیں یا نہیں قبول کی جاتی، پہلے کہا گیا اور ان لوگوں کا بیان جن کی شہادت قبول کی جاتی ہے یا قبول نہیں کی جاتی بعد میں کیا گیا۔ صاحب عنایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ شہادت کے رد ہونے کی اصل اور بنیاد تہمت ہے چنانچه صاحب شریعت کا ارشاد لا شهادة متهم متہم آدمی کی شہادت معتبر نہیں ہے۔ دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہادت ایک خبر ہے جو صدق اور کذب کا احتمال رکھتی ہے مگر حجت شرعی اسکا اسوقت ثابت ہوگا۔ جب اس میں صدق کی جانب راج ہو اور تہمت کے ساتھ جانب صدق
Chapter on who accepts his testimony and who does not. Translation: This chapter deals with those whose testimony is accepted and those whose testimony is not accepted. Commentary: The previous section discussed the things in which testimony is heard and the things in which testimony is not heard. After completing that, in this chapter, the people whose testimony is accepted and those whose testimony is not accepted will be discussed. But since the things in which testimony is accepted or not accepted are the locus (mahall) of testimony, and the locus is a condition, and the condition precedes the conditioned, therefore the things were mentioned first and the people are mentioned later. Sahib al-'Inayah said: The basis for rejection of testimony is accusation (tuhma). The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'No testimony for an accused person.' Another proof: testimony is a report that may be true or false, but it becomes a legal proof only when truth is preponderant, and with accusation, the side of truth is (weakened).
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This is the beginning of a new chapter in the Book of Testimony. The passage is introductory, explaining the structure and rationale for the order of topics. The chapter heading states that it covers who can and cannot testify. The commentator explains why the previous section (about what things testimony is accepted for) came before this chapter: because the thing testified about (the locus) is a condition for testimony, and conditions logically precede the conditioned (the people). He then cites a principle from al-'Inayah that the basis for rejecting testimony is accusation (tuhma), supported by the prophetic hadith 'No testimony for an accused person' and reasoning that testimony is a report where truth must outweigh falsehood, and accusation undermines truthfulness.

Key Terms
محل (mahall): The locus or subject matter of testimony; here it refers to the thing about which testimony is given.
شرط (shart): Condition; something whose presence is necessary for the validity of another thing.
مشروط (mashrut): The conditioned; the thing that depends on the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the subject matter of testimony discussed before the witnesses?
A) Because the subject matter is more important
B) Because the subject matter is a condition and conditions precede the conditioned
C) Because the witnesses are less important
D) Because the subject matter is easier to define

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi legal texts, conditions logically precede the conditioned; thus the subject matter of testimony is discussed before the witnesses.

The Principle of Tuhma as the Basis for Rejecting Testimony
Introduction of the fundamental principle for rejecting testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that tuhma (accusation) is the basis for rejecting testimony, cite the prophetic hadith, and explain the reasoning using the nature of testimony as a report.

Core Idea
Accusation (tuhma) undermines the preponderance of truth in testimony, making it inadmissible.

After establishing the logical order of topics, the passage introduces a fundamental principle: the basis for rejecting testimony is tuhma (accusation or suspicion). This is supported by the prophetic hadith 'No testimony for an accused person' and a rational argument that testimony is a report where truth must be preponderant, and tuhma weakens truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key principle
Sahib al-'Inayah states: 'The basis for rejection of testimony is accusation (tuhma).' This is the core principle.
Tuhma includes any suspicion that may cause the witness to lie.
2
Cite the textual proof
The Prophet (ﷺ) said: 'لا شهادة متهم' (No testimony for an accused person). This hadith directly supports the principle.
This hadith is considered a strong proof in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Analyze the rational proof
Testimony is a report (khabar) that can be true or false. For it to be legal proof (hujja shar'iyya), truth must be preponderant (rajih). Tuhma makes falsehood preponderant or at least prevents truth from being preponderant.
Hanafis use this reasoning to extend the principle beyond the explicit hadith.
4
Understand the implication
Whenever there is a legitimate suspicion (tuhma) that a witness might not be truthful, their testimony is rejected. This includes various forms of bias, interest, or enmity.
The details of what constitutes tuhma are elaborated in later sections.

Source Text

تہمت (tuhma): Accusation or suspicion that impugns a witness's credibility; the basis for rejecting testimony.
صدق (sidq): Truthfulness; testimony is considered a report where truth is the default but must be preponderant.
خبر (khabar): A report or statement that can be true or false; testimony is classified as a khabar.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle 'لا شهادة متهم' (No testimony for an accused person) is the foundational basis for rejecting testimony, rooted in the need for preponderance of truth.

Testimony on Ownership by Possession: The Exception of Discerning Slaves
Ruling on testimony of ownership by possession with an intra-Hanafi dispute on discerning slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for testifying to ownership based on possession, explain the exception for discerning slaves, and compare the two Hanafi positions on this issue.

Core Idea
Possession proves ownership for goods, but for a discerning slave, the slave's self-control breaks the link.

In Hanafi testimony law, seeing an item in someone's hand is usually enough to testify that they own it. But what if the 'item' is a human being who can speak and act on their own? This lesson untangles the rule, the exception, and a key disagreement within the Hanafi school about whether a discerning slave's possession still counts as evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the default ruling: seeing an item in someone's possession permits testimony that the possessor owns it. Note the basis: possession is a strong indicator (dalīl) of ownership.
This applies to inanimate goods like cloth.
2
Identify the exception
Locate the exclusion: 'except the male and female slave' (sawi al-‘abd wa al-amah). Understand why – because a discerning slave has independent control and can claim freedom, thus undermining the possessor's apparent ownership.
Discerning means capable of independent speech and action.
3
Uncover the intra-Hanafi dispute
Find the narration from Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn that the exception does not apply: even for discerning slaves, possession is still evidence. Recognize that the author presents this as the relied-upon position.
This view uses qiyas on clothing – possession is possession.
4
Analyse the qiyas (analogy)
Break down the analogy: asl (original case) = clothing, where possession proves ownership because cloth has no will. far‘ (new case) = discerning slave. The illa (effective cause) is that possession is a reliable external sign. The opponent's objection: the slave's will breaks the illa. The Hanafi response: possession is still a strong indicator, and the slave's claim can be addressed separately.
Argument type: qiyās jaliyy (clear analogy).
5
Determine the thamarah (practical consequence)
Apply the dispute: If a dispute arises between a claimant and the possessor of a slave, whose word is accepted? Under the majority view, the possessor due to apparent possession; under the exception view, the slave's own statement may override. The mufta bihi is that the possessor's testimony is valid.
The slave’s claim to freedom is a separate issue.

Source Text
